<div id="review_1709886782" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1709886782" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1709886782?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20429899-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=stela-e%C3%B0a-l%C3%A1ni">stela-eða-láni</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=books-most-loved">books-most-loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=mina-favoritb%C3%B6cker">mina-favoritböcker</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1709886782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4706469652053705411"><u>
  <b>Phenomenal Favola--Due Amici</b>
</u><br/><br/>This is the second of a tetralogy called the "Neopolitan Novels," by Italian novelist Elena Ferrante (pseudonym), who says she considers the four volumes to constitute one novel. The books were so popular in Italy that the periodical publications regularly engaged in a game of speculation on the author's true identity.<br/><br/>The books center on the lives and friendship of two girls from Naples, Italy, Elena Greco (called sometimes "Lenù") and Raffaella ("Lila") Cerullo. </span>
  <span id="freeText4706469652053705411" style="display:none"><u>
  <b>Phenomenal Favola--Due Amici</b>
</u><br /><br />This is the second of a tetralogy called the "Neopolitan Novels," by Italian novelist Elena Ferrante (pseudonym), who says she considers the four volumes to constitute one novel. The books were so popular in Italy that the periodical publications regularly engaged in a game of speculation on the author's true identity.<br /><br />The books center on the lives and friendship of two girls from Naples, Italy, Elena Greco (called sometimes "Lenù") and Raffaella ("Lila") Cerullo. Both are intelligent and precocious young students in the first book called <i>My Brilliant Friend</i>, which takes them up to 16 years old. This one, <i>The Story of a New Name</i> carries them to their mid-20s. Elena is the narrator, but it's truly about both of them, and all that relationship entails through the years, including intellectual and sexual competition and envy, and support for each other as they attempt to rise above their poor, vulgar and sometimes violent neighborhood on the Naples outskirts. <br /><br />In some ways, Ms. Ferrante's writing reminds me of that by the Norwegian Karl Ove Knaussgard in his six-volume <i>My Struggle</i>, so conversational and existential without being overly gloomy. I can't pin down exactly why but I find these books absolutely absorbing and fascinating. I want to keep reading... and reading.... It's like a dip into the soul of the authors, their daily lives, and the intriguing neighborhood interrelationships, friendships, as the authors call you forth, in examining their own lives, to examine yours, your childhood, childhood friends, being an adult, growing older, how sad things turn out for many who could not escape their surroundings, your recollections of certain things but haziness on others, the meaning of art and lit in life <i>and to a full life</i>, the meaning of your upbringing in your life, the ways you are like and differ from your parents when you become a parent, the place you grew up, how it felt to be isolated, in love with someone, to lose them to another, in lust, puppy love, your first sexual encounter, how you felt upon seeing someone again or for the first time in years and years. The Neopolitan novels are full of good and evil and eccentric characters. <br /><br />I found the first novel a little tedious at times because it mostly involved elementary age kids. Unlike the Karl Ove novels, it is necessary to read these in order. For this second one, I was all in, finding it simply jaw-dropping at times. I'm already halfway through the 3d now, I've enjoyed them so much.<br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4706469652053705411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1257976560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1257976560" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1257976560?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=italy-theme-setting">italy-theme-setting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2015">best-of-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1257976560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2708007143593171246">It is the early-mid 1960s and Naples is experiencing an economic and cultural renaissance: the post-war boom has created a new consumer class, with fancy shoe boutiques staffed by pretty girls dressed up like Jackie O. In university halls, students speak of the two Germanys, Indochina, nuclear arms, and Communism. <br/><br/>But not everything has changed. In the darker neighborhoods on the outskirts of the city, where violence is an accepted means of communication and a woman’s worth is tallied by first h</span>
  <span id="freeText2708007143593171246" style="display:none">It is the early-mid 1960s and Naples is experiencing an economic and cultural renaissance: the post-war boom has created a new consumer class, with fancy shoe boutiques staffed by pretty girls dressed up like Jackie O. In university halls, students speak of the two Germanys, Indochina, nuclear arms, and Communism. <br /><br />But not everything has changed. In the darker neighborhoods on the outskirts of the city, where violence is an accepted means of communication and a woman’s worth is tallied by first her father, then her husband, tradition vies with progress. <br /><br />It is here we left Lila on her wedding day, at the end of Book One of Ferrante’s Neapolitan Novels, <i>My Brilliant Friend</i>. Elena watched from the sidelines as her best friend sashayed into a life of comfort, buoyed by her husband Stefano’s economic success. <br /><br />But how quickly fortunes shift. Book Two, <i>The Story of a New Name</i>, is still Lina and Elena’s story. The new name belongs to the girl who exchanged her father’s name for her husband’s yet remains confined to the old way of life, while a new life is granted to the plump, shy, awkward, girl who is able to continue her education. Womanhood awaits them both, but we see how conflicted Elena has become, feeling ever in the shadow of Lila’s magnetic beauty. The day of her marriage, Elena helps Lila prepare: <br /><blockquote>I washed her with slow, careful gestures, first letting her squat in the tub, then asking her to stand up: I still have in my ears the sound of the dripping water, and the impression that the copper of the tub had a consistency not different from Lila’s flesh, which was smooth, solid, calm. I had a confusion of feelings and thoughts: embrace her, weep with her, kiss her, pull her hair, laugh, pretend to sexual experience and instruct her in a learned voice, distancing her with words just at the moment of greatest closeness.<br /><br />But in the end there was only the hostile thought that I was washing her, from her hair to the soles of her feet, early in the morning, just so that Stefano could sully her in the course of the night. I imagined her naked as she was at that moment, entwined with her husband, in the bed in the new house, while the train clattered under their windows and his violent flesh entered her with a sharp blow, like the cork pushed by the palm into the neck of a wine bottle. And it suddenly seemed to me that the only remedy against the pain I was feeling, that I would feel, was to find a corner secluded enough so that Antonio could do to me, at the same time, the exact same thing.</blockquote><br />I posited that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1220870483&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>My Brilliant Friend</i>
</a> is a novel of power; <i>The Story of a New Name</i> is about trust. In the opening scene, set some fifty years after Lila’s wedding, Elena betrays her friend’s trust, saying <i>“I couldn’t stand feeling Lila on me and in me, even now that I was esteemed myself, even now that I had a life outside of Naples.”</i> She dumps the journals Lila had given her for safekeeping into the Arno River, but then she turns back and tells Lila’s story to us, her readers, so that we’ll remember Lila, and the old neighborhood, forever. <br /><br />Lila and Stefano’s marriage is built on sand, but it is a castle they manage to rebuild over and over again in the early years. Lila trusts her cleverness and beauty will protect what she most wants: control; Stefano trusts his position as husband and provider will allow him the same. Elena knows better than to put her faith in Lila, but she does, time and again, until her best friend shatters her heart. The young man whose affections she has been pining for since childhood turns his brooding eye to Lila, the young bride. The affair becomes Lila’s undoing, while at the same time Elena begins her slow rise, far from Naples and whirlpool of tradition and family. She escapes Lila’s fate:<br /><blockquote>I saw clearly the mothers of the old neighborhood. They were nervous, they were acquiescent. They were silent, with tight lips and stooping shoulders, or they yelled terrible insults at the children who harassed them. Extremely thin, with hollow eyes and cheeks, or with broad behinds, swollen ankles, heavy chests, they lugged shopping bags and small children who clung to their skirts. They had been consumed by the bodies of husbands, fathers, brothers, whom they ultimately came to resemble, because of their labors or the arrival of old age, of illness. When did that transformation begin? With housework? With pregnancies? With beatings?</blockquote> <br />But Elena cannot escape the dream that is Lila, the girl whom she knows to be more intelligent, quicker, more articulate—the real scholar. Elena handwrites a draft of a novel and offers it as a university graduation gift to a boyfriend, who passes it along to his mother, a book editor. And suddenly she is swept up in success. But it is Lila’s spirit that wrote Elena's book, even though it came from Elena’s hands. Elena discovers <i>The Blue Fairy</i>, a short novel Lila had written as a child, and realizes Lila’s words and voice are <blockquote>the secret heart of my book. Anyone who wanted to know what gave it warmth and what the origin was of the strong but invisible thread that joined the sentences would have had to go back to that child’s packet, ten notebook pages, . . . the brightly colored cover, the title and not even a signature.</blockquote> And in a gesture of trust and love for her friend, Elena returns the story to Lila, admitting, <i>“I read it again and discovered that, without realizing it, I’ve always had it in my mind. That’s where my book comes from.”</i> It is the story of a new name. <br /><br />Yet would seem too late for redemption from Elena. Lila is lost, a fallen woman, the transformation Elena observed and dreaded a few years earlier in the wives of the old neighborhood has overcome her friend. Lila tosses her story into the flames and Elena leave Naples. <br /><br />But Elena and Lila are still young, only in their mid-twenties, and there is still so much of their stories yet to tell. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2708007143593171246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1224727518" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1224727518" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1224727518?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1224727518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17057045073498087864">This Europa editions' cover is silly, even more unsuitable than the cover of the first book of the series. Ferrante's focus is not on romance at all -- there is nothing romantic about the desperate, grasping lives these people lead -- her scope is epic: social and political.<br/><br/>In my review of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels___1_' title='My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels, #1) by Elena Ferrante' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Brilliant Friend</a> I noted that flight was not yet an option for the girls. Even if it becomes so, the impossibility of fleeing your origins hovers over them in this installment. While reading one section, I </span>
  <span id="freeText17057045073498087864" style="display:none">This Europa editions' cover is silly, even more unsuitable than the cover of the first book of the series. Ferrante's focus is not on romance at all -- there is nothing romantic about the desperate, grasping lives these people lead -- her scope is epic: social and political.<br /><br />In my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels___1_" title="My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels, #1) by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">My Brilliant Friend</a> I noted that flight was not yet an option for the girls. Even if it becomes so, the impossibility of fleeing your origins hovers over them in this installment. While reading one section, I was reminded of the 1997 movie <i>The Boxer</i> (starring Daniel Day-Lewis and Emily Watson) with its masculine code-of-"honor" within its own working-class neighborhood, a code that uses beatings to terrorize its own.<br /><br />As with the first novel, the ending arrives abruptly. With both books, there are several blank pages following the end: a trick so you won't slow down as I might've, not wanting to get to the end yet; or a reminder that there's more to come? <br /><br />I'm not sure I will get to the third book as quickly as I did this, as the translated fourth--and last--is not due here until September, forcing me to pace myself.<br /><br />*<br />Added <b>July 14, 2016</b>:<br /><br />I just came across this article about the intentional 'badness' of the covers. (That hadn't even crossed my mind.) <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.theatlantic.com/entertainment/archive/2016/07/elena-ferrante-covers-bad-no-good/488732/">http://www.theatlantic.com/entertainm...</a><br /><br />'Ferrante’s publisher even expressed concern to Slate that “many people didn’t understand the game we we’re playing, that of, let’s say, dressing an extremely refined story with a touch of vulgarity.” Certainly, readers aren’t required to enjoy the cloying sensibility of the images just because they’re intentionally bad, and because Ferrante herself chose them.' </span>
  <a data-text-id="17057045073498087864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1746546089?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781483080529" href="/book/show/25912479-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=italian">italian</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1746546089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer102887926786381117">4.5 stars<br/><br/>This, the second instalment of the Neapolitan series, held my attention in a vice grip from the first minute. It continues into young adulthood the story of the friendship of Lenu and Lila - a complex, competitive connection which spurs on much DRAMA.<br/><br/>Yes, drama, drama, drama - so many fights, beatings, broken hearts, betrayals, pregnancies (it made me laugh a little how they are always surprised at what results from repeated unprotected sex) and oh so much more! Such high highs, such lo</span>
  <span id="freeText102887926786381117" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br /><br />This, the second instalment of the Neapolitan series, held my attention in a vice grip from the first minute. It continues into young adulthood the story of the friendship of Lenu and Lila - a complex, competitive connection which spurs on much DRAMA.<br /><br />Yes, drama, drama, drama - so many fights, beatings, broken hearts, betrayals, pregnancies (it made me laugh a little how they are always surprised at what results from repeated unprotected sex) and oh so much more! Such high highs, such low lows. (And a few frankly shocking moments, including one very icky one that I hope is explained somehow in the next book.) At times I felt I was following an Italian soap opera. <br /><br />However, I was totally drawn in, fascinated at the arc of Lenu and Lila's lives, in awe of how the author is able to to constantly get into the psychology of the moment, and make me care about the cast of characters I've come to know. The struggles of making it out of the neighbourhood, doing something that counts, being the best, education, rising above poverty and the stigma of the neapolitan accent are all drivers that propel the action. And, of course, love.<br /><br />As I said, I'm all in. Can't wait to start the next!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="102887926786381117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_764266607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/764266607" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7574349-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391787862p2/7574349.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/764266607?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/7574349-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer764266607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer499033038343104323">"Partly because her work describes domestic experiences – such as vivid sexual jealousy and other forms of shame – that are underexplored in fiction, Ferrante’s reputation is soaring, especially among women (Zadie Smith, Mona Simpson and Jhumpa Lahiri are fans). Her writing has a powerful intimacy – as if her characters, to paraphrase Ralph Waldo Emerson, are the lenses through which we read our own minds. The novelist Claire Messud emailed, “When you write to me and say you love her work, I hav</span>
  <span id="freeText499033038343104323" style="display:none">"Partly because her work describes domestic experiences – such as vivid sexual jealousy and other forms of shame – that are underexplored in fiction, Ferrante’s reputation is soaring, especially among women (Zadie Smith, Mona Simpson and Jhumpa Lahiri are fans). Her writing has a powerful intimacy – as if her characters, to paraphrase Ralph Waldo Emerson, are the lenses through which we read our own minds. The novelist Claire Messud emailed, “When you write to me and say you love her work, I have a moment where I think, ‘But … Elena is my friend! My private relationship with her, so intense and so true, is one that nobody else can fully know!’ It’s strange – and rare – to feel proprietary of a book, or a writer, in that way.”"<br /><br />"In a 2003 written interview, Ferrante said, “The true reader, I think, searches not for the brittle face of the author in flesh and blood” but instead for “the naked physiognomy that remains in every effective word”. Whoever Ferrante is, in the novel she is free to invent, to fabricate, to play, to revisit old wounds, to be less than beautiful. This is what writing can do: create a space for the savage within, for the contradictory and the wild, and make it real. There may be no consolation except the art itself, but what a pleasure for those of us who get to read it. I would not want to forget what Ferrante herself so eloquently stated in one of her letters: the mystery of literature is in some ways its difference from the person who wrote it, the unfathomable effacement of self that leads to its creation."<br /><br />Meghan O'Rourke in The Guardian<br /><br /><i>To create a space for the savage within, for the contradictory and the wild, and make it real.</i><br /><br />I could not express what these Neapolitan novels do any better than Meghan O'Rourke does in her fiercely intelligent and perceptive review of Elena Ferrante's novels for the Guardian. <br /><br />You will simply never <i>experience</i> women characters in this way anywhere else. You get to read and feel the female psyche with more vibrancy and complexity and beauty than you can ever hope to find in a literary work. And the novels <i>never</i> feel literary. The characters feel as if they are coming into being right in front of your eyes, sentence by sentence, page by page, in a electrifying mess of living matter.<br /><br />One of the most thrilling reading experiences I've had in recent years.<br /><br />Meghan O'Rourke's review can be read in full here:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.theguardian.com/books/2014/oct/31/elena-ferrante-literary-sensation-nobody-knows">http://www.theguardian.com/books/2014...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="499033038343104323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1749701420" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1749701420" />
    <a title="PorshaJo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo"><img alt="PorshaJo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451425289p2/5464046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1749701420?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PorshaJo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PorshaJo" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo">PorshaJo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challenge">challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads">challengereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads-2016">challengereads-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1749701420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2033783337418863541">Rating a 4.5<br/><br/>Elena Greco and Lila Cerullo are friends, and through these books, you get the details of their lives and their friendship. You do not get a glimpse of their lives, you hear the minutiae of their daily lives. And you know what...it's utterly fascinating. This is a book to be savored slowly. There really is so much going on and there are so many people from the neighborhood that move in and out of the story. The story packs in so much - love, betrayal, friendship, marriage, adultery, </span>
  <span id="freeText2033783337418863541" style="display:none">Rating a 4.5<br /><br />Elena Greco and Lila Cerullo are friends, and through these books, you get the details of their lives and their friendship. You do not get a glimpse of their lives, you hear the minutiae of their daily lives. And you know what...it's utterly fascinating. This is a book to be savored slowly. There really is so much going on and there are so many people from the neighborhood that move in and out of the story. The story packs in so much - love, betrayal, friendship, marriage, adultery, internal conflict, abuse, suicide, and so much more.<br /><br />Book two in this amazing series picks up right where book one ends, with the marriage of Lila to Stefano. And it moves right into their wedding night, onto the honeymoon, and into their daily lives. The girls go away to the beach for the summer for Lila to swim so she can get and stay pregnant. Doctors orders. I'll not discuss anymore of what happens and spoil this story for anyone.<br /><br />I highly suggest these books to anyone to read. They are so detailed, fascinating, and just beautiful. I listened to this one via audio and the narrator is wonderful. Normally, I loose interest in a series of books. But not with this one. I just got the print of book three (my library doesn't have audio of book three, darn). There are a lot of people in the books, and some have nicknames, or various nicknames. So at times, it can be hard to remember who is who. In the print books, there is a sort of family tree of the neighborhood which explains everyone and how they might be connected. I did not want to read the books back to back and as there are only four books, I want them to last. But I don't wait to wait for the next, now that I *know* who is who. I see myself reading these books again and again.<br /><br />I went to my library yesterday and picked up book three in the series. The librarian had no idea about the book or the series. I was shocked. I told her all about them....so she could suggest this wonderful series to many others to enjoy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2033783337418863541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1473777477" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1473777477" />
    <a title="Doug Bradshaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/808994-doug-bradshaw"><img alt="Doug Bradshaw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548429066p2/808994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1473777477?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug Bradshaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug Bradshaw" href="/user/show/808994-doug-bradshaw">Doug Bradshaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781609451479" href="/book/show/19185150-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1473777477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4873688311015847288">Rarely have I experienced a more personal and honest picture of someone’s private inner thoughts, the mechanics of friendship and growing up, with all of the pressures of deeply ingrained habits and customs of a different culture and generation: If you are chosen by a young male from a successful family and asked to marry, who are you to turn him down? It doesn’t matter that you aren’t attracted to him. If you were to say no, your father would beat you and disown you. But don’t worry, over time </span>
  <span id="freeText4873688311015847288" style="display:none">Rarely have I experienced a more personal and honest picture of someone’s private inner thoughts, the mechanics of friendship and growing up, with all of the pressures of deeply ingrained habits and customs of a different culture and generation: If you are chosen by a young male from a successful family and asked to marry, who are you to turn him down? It doesn’t matter that you aren’t attracted to him. If you were to say no, your father would beat you and disown you. But don’t worry, over time you will learn to love him and you will have all of the things in life that are important, a modern roomy apartment, jewelry, beautiful clothes and money to spend on frivolous things. <br /><br />Here’s a little sample of the writing. While I was reading it, I thought it felt a little like it was a translation and yet, over time, as I got used to it, I just loved the writing: <br /><br /><i> “They began to hold hands without hiding it, with an offensive shamelessness, as if they had decided that with us it wasn’t worth pretending. They often quarreled jokingly, then grabbed each other, hit each other, held each other tight, tumbled on the sand together. When we were walking, if they spotted an abandoned hut, an old bath house reduced to its foundations, a path that got lost among the wild vegetation, they decided like children to go exploring and didn’t invite us to follow. They went off with him in the lead, she behind, in silence. When they lay in the sun, they lessened the distance between them as much as possible. At first they were satisfied with the slight contact of shoulders, their arms, legs, feet just grazing. Later, returning from that interminable daily swim, they lay beside each other on ’s towel, which was bigger, and soon, with a natural gesture, ---- put his arm around her shoulders, she rested her head on his chest. They even, once, went so far as to kiss on the lips, a light, quick kiss. I thought: she’s mad, they’re mad. If someone from Naples who knows----sees them?” <br /><br />“Her words were very beautiful, mine are only a summary. If she had confided it to me then, in the taxi, I would have suffered even more, because I would have recognized in her fulfillment the reverse of my emptiness. I would have understood that she had come to something that I thought I knew, that I had believed I felt for him, and that, instead, I didn’t know, and perhaps would never know, except in a weak, muted form. I would have understood that she wasn’t playing a summer game for fun but that a violent feeling was growing inside her and would overwhelm her.”</i><br /><br />This second book takes place mainly when the two main female characters are between the ages of 16 and 23, when one of the two gets married at a young age to a successful businessman who gives her all of the material niceties, while the other struggles to ignore these things, the more expected and normal path of getting married and having babies, and sacrifices to finish high school and then attend the university. Through all of this, the two remain very close, are affected by each other in almost every way, second guessing their own lives and wondering if the other has made the right decisions. <br /><br />This book is not a stand alone book. It needs to be read after reading the first book. I’m on to the third book now. I hope you’ll read them.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4873688311015847288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2774458638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12661047136285098930">In this second book in the series we see Elena and Lila as they move on in their lives, no longer truly friends, but still held together by the same almost inexplicable bond they have had since they were small.<br/><br/>The whole novel is told from Elena's point of view and sometimes it is hard to understand why Lila does some of the things she does. Elena on the other hand is constantly struggling to improve herself and to achieve a better life. Sadly she has a very poor opinion of her own self worth and</span>
  <span id="freeText12661047136285098930" style="display:none">In this second book in the series we see Elena and Lila as they move on in their lives, no longer truly friends, but still held together by the same almost inexplicable bond they have had since they were small.<br /><br />The whole novel is told from Elena's point of view and sometimes it is hard to understand why Lila does some of the things she does. Elena on the other hand is constantly struggling to improve herself and to achieve a better life. Sadly she has a very poor opinion of her own self worth and spends a lot of her time in a state of confusion. Sometimes those chapters in the book seemed a little long!<br /><br />A lot does happen along the way - lovers come and go, wives get beaten, babies are born, friendships get broken - all of this written in Ferrante's beautiful style. The ending is stunning and totally unexpected, designed specifically to make the reader want to jump straight into the next book. I am going to do just that.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1056488926" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1056488926" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1056488926?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenny (Reading Envy) by: </span>
      Harper's Magazine October 2014
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2016">read2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-italy">location-italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=women-in-translation">women-in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1056488926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18110299980020131819">I read the first book in this series, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels__1_' title='My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels #1) by Elena Ferrante' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Brilliant Friend</a>, over a year ago, but always thought I liked it enough to read the next one sometime. I just wasn't in a hurry. (You can read my review of book one <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1056488917' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>.)<br/><br/>I'm certainly glad I finally dug into this one. Where the first book was about childhood and adolescence, with the microcosmic world of girlhood friendships (and conflicts) and aspirations, this book focuses on the two women, Lila and Elena, in their upper teen years. For Lila, this is a</span>
  <span id="freeText18110299980020131819" style="display:none">I read the first book in this series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels__1_" title="My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels #1) by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">My Brilliant Friend</a>, over a year ago, but always thought I liked it enough to read the next one sometime. I just wasn't in a hurry. (You can read my review of book one <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1056488917" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)<br /><br />I'm certainly glad I finally dug into this one. Where the first book was about childhood and adolescence, with the microcosmic world of girlhood friendships (and conflicts) and aspirations, this book focuses on the two women, Lila and Elena, in their upper teen years. For Lila, this is an early marriage. For Elena, this is schooling. I think for both of them, there is this realization of the realities of the world they are actually living in, from the poverty to the place of violence within families... there is a moment I remember where the way they see the women in their little village changes when they realize they also are likely to end up trapped. <blockquote>"I saw clearly the mothers of the old neighborhood. They were nervous, they were acquiescent. They were silent, with tight lips and stooping shoulders, or they yelled terrible insults at the children who harassed them. Extremely thin, with hollow eyes and cheeks, or with broad behinds, swollen ankles, heavy chests, they lugged shopping bags and small children who clung to their skirts and wanted to be picked up. And, good God, they were ten, at most twenty years older than me. .. They had been consumed by the bodies of husbands, fathers, brothers, whom they ultimately came to resemble, because of their labors or the arrival of old age, of illness. When did that transformation begin? With housework? With pregnancies? With beatings? Would Lila be misshapen like Nunzia? ... And would by body, too, one day be ruined by the emergence of not only my mother's body but my father's? And would all that I was learning at school dissolve, would the neighborhood prevail again, the cadences, the manners, everything be confounded in black mire?"</blockquote>Elena struggles to fit in; her romances don't work out and Lila even as a married woman seems to have more allure. Lila doesn't struggle to fit in, and it is almost worse for her. She does not make friends with her new family and they become an entirely new set of enemies, in many ways. <blockquote>"That people, even more than things, lost their boundaries and overflowed into shapelessness is what most frightened Lila in the course of her life."</blockquote>What I always love about Ferrante, something I particularly noticed since I was also reading another book about two female friends as they grow up that I didn't connect with nearly as well, is that she somehow provides us with just enough of the inner workings of the characters to really feel their agony, but keeps enough of it from us for some of their decisions to seem unpredictable. They are so human, they are so defined by their surroundings, and she captures how guilt can keep people in poverty even if they would have the ability to move upwards and outwards. The violence in this community remains as startling as it did in the first book.<blockquote>"For your whole life you love people and you never really know who they are."</blockquote>As Elena expands her circles to intellectual discourse in Pisa, an interesting topic comes up, a debate of whether or not the USA should be charged with war crimes for the bombs dropped on Hiroshima and Nagasaki. The timing of this book is during and WWII during the 50s and 60s, and it really only shows up in terms of the poverty, the men coming back with unnamed ptsd, and some of the discussions going on. But it is a significant movement forward for Elena, who explores ideas of communism and socialism and anarchy within the rather safe boundaries of the academy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18110299980020131819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2534921337" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2534921337" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2534921337?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2534921337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6343555650973167823">The story continues, and it is still as riveting as the first volume was. This book begins where the previous, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35036409.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels___1_' title='My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels, #1) by Elena Ferrante' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Brilliant Friend</a>, ended. Where violence was brimming in the previous novel, it pours out in this one. <br/><br/>Lila and Lenu are growing up, it is the late 1960s and although the story primarily still takes place in the poor neighbourhood in Naples, Lila through marriage ascends the social ladder while Lenu is still struggling to follow suit, but through education.<br/><br/>Reading this book, I was so </span>
  <span id="freeText6343555650973167823" style="display:none">The story continues, and it is still as riveting as the first volume was. This book begins where the previous, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35036409.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels___1_" title="My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels, #1) by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">My Brilliant Friend</a>, ended. Where violence was brimming in the previous novel, it pours out in this one. <br /><br />Lila and Lenu are growing up, it is the late 1960s and although the story primarily still takes place in the poor neighbourhood in Naples, Lila through marriage ascends the social ladder while Lenu is still struggling to follow suit, but through education.<br /><br />Reading this book, I was so surprised at how children in this era seem to grow up fast, or were forced to grow up so quickly. By the age of sixteen, most of the characters assume great responsibilities and a lot is expected from them. I also cannot take for granted that the people in this book are from a poor neighbourhood and the struggles of life typically force individuals to assume certain responsibilities sooner than they ought to.<br /><br />Though the story is still narrated by Lenu/Elena, Lila still holds quite a portion of the story, so that their stories are so mingled, even when they are no longer as close as they were before. Which speaks of the imprint those who are close to us leave onto us, and the competitive nature of life as we continuously compare ourselves to our peers in order to measure our progress, although in Lenu's case it is borderline obsessive. <br /><br /><i>"I understood that I had arrived there full of pride and realized that—in good faith, certainly, with affection—I had made that whole journey mainly to show her what she had lost and what I had won. But she had known from the moment I appeared, and now, risking tensions with her workmates, and fines, she was explaining to me that I had won nothing, that in the world there is nothing to win, that her life was full of varied and foolish adventures as much as mine, and that time simply slipped away without any meaning, and it was good just to see each other every so often to hear the mad sound of the brain of one echo in the mad sound of the brain of the other.."</i><br /><br />Although Elena progresses and prospers, she still clings to her friend while using her life as a yard stick against hers. <br /><br />Ferrante's story telling captures the flow of life, in its tragedies and triumphs, its passions, betrayal, defeats, resilience, drawing in the reader so that by the time this ended I couldn't wait to go on with the next book in the series. And I definitely shall.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6343555650973167823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9472704-linda?shelf=5-star-winners">5-star-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9472704-linda?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3257243704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9263323611279613466">The Story of A New Name just threw me over the cliff. By the time that I got to the final sentence on Page 471, I barely even knew my own name.......<br/><br/>If you've been following the HBO series of the same name, you must be familiar with this outstanding series filmed in Italy with subtitles. Not your usual brew? Think again. <br/><br/>We're hunkered down on one of the curbs lining the dusty streets in an older section of Naples. We view two little girls clutching their dolls and leaving echoes of their laught</span>
  <span id="freeText9263323611279613466" style="display:none">The Story of A New Name just threw me over the cliff. By the time that I got to the final sentence on Page 471, I barely even knew my own name.......<br /><br />If you've been following the HBO series of the same name, you must be familiar with this outstanding series filmed in Italy with subtitles. Not your usual brew? Think again. <br /><br />We're hunkered down on one of the curbs lining the dusty streets in an older section of Naples. We view two little girls clutching their dolls and leaving echoes of their laughter behind. Lila and Lenu are inseparable. From early morning until evening sets in, the girls amuse themselves with dreaming of walking miles and miles to the beach. Just to see it. Just to experience it.<br /><br />But life leans hard on them both. Lenu will pursue her studies diligently with donated books and scholarships. Lila, just as bright, must forgo high school because of family commitments in their shoe shop. It's the 1950's and, for the most part, little girls just grew into women who followed a particular role. Lila marries an up and coming grocery store owner, Stefano, at the tender age of sixteen. When Lila finds out a secret on her wedding day about Stefano, the marriage is doomed.<br /><br />The Story of A New Name is voiced by Lenu as she reflects upon the vast transformations that occur in the lives of these two young women. We become more and more aware of dysfunctions within families, friendships, male and female relationships, and the city of Naples and the countryside. Jealousies and resentments weave their way into this once solid friendship and bleed through to staining the future for both of them.<br /><br />I must tell you that this book is very graphic at times and explicit. These characters reflect the time period and are highly emotional and electrically charged in their relationships. But consider the era in which they lived and the social rankings of male and female ingrained since birth. Elena Ferrante has done a superb job of portraying these very flawed individuals and their reactions to life. You'll keep turning pages for fear of leaving these characters frozen in time until the next chapter. Seriously......<br /><br />The book follows the series and the series follows the book even into the dialogue. Now's the time to sit back with a grabber of a read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9263323611279613466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2019020036" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2019020036" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2019020036?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781925240016" href="/book/show/18393091-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2019020036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8318516759317723692"><i>The Story of a New Name</i> is a novel about frustration. Frustration is the underlying motivation for each character, manifesting in different ways for the individuals, but in repetitive and self-reinforcing patterns that in turn create a chain of similar outcomes within the community: violence, broken marriages, and suffocating, unhappy lives.<br/><br/>At the centre of the novel, though viewed from a somewhat removed position is Lila’s frustration, her failure of self-actualisation arising from her early ma</span>
  <span id="freeText8318516759317723692" style="display:none"><i>The Story of a New Name</i> is a novel about frustration. Frustration is the underlying motivation for each character, manifesting in different ways for the individuals, but in repetitive and self-reinforcing patterns that in turn create a chain of similar outcomes within the community: violence, broken marriages, and suffocating, unhappy lives.<br /><br />At the centre of the novel, though viewed from a somewhat removed position is Lila’s frustration, her failure of self-actualisation arising from her early marriage and truncated education. For much of this novel she is a thoroughly unlikable character who can be seen as the cause of much misery and suffering. But she is also the victim of her own circumstances, the pressures of her environment and her own ignorance. <br /><br />If there is a societal lesson that can be learned from this story it is of the importance of education. The violence of the neighbourhood and its culture of vindictiveness seem to stem from a general ignorance of the wider world, an inability to act with empathy and see past petty concerns. Elena’s own path to education seems impossibly difficult, threatened not only by envious attempts at sabotage but by the passive friction of expectation and ignorance even of the possibilities that life can offer. This is a difficult cycle to break.<br /><br />Driving the plot are several love triangles that at one time become so convoluted and incestuous as to border on absurdity. However even as the story threatens to descend into soap-opera territory, there is always an unexpected development or insight that rescues it. It becomes evident towards the end of the book that what we are reading is likely to be far more autobiographical than fictitious. Perhaps it is this basis in reality that repels the cliches that would otherwise arise so naturally if we were witnessing the contrived development of fictional characters rather than the messy lives of real people. It is also this knowledge that lends weight to the suffering these characters endure. The incidents of sexual and domestic violence, of fear and heartbreak, of personal resignation to lead eternally unfulfilling lives - these are no longer hypothetical, and become all the more tragic and disturbing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8318516759317723692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1457918667" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1457918667" />
    <a title="Bianca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29843800-bianca"><img alt="Bianca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589154116p2/29843800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1457918667?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bianca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bianca" href="/user/show/29843800-bianca">Bianca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781489055224" href="/book/show/30012742-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29843800-bianca?shelf=italian">italian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1457918667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10490303163645764471">How does one even begin to review this second volume in the Neapolitan series?<br/><br/>What an incredible, mesmerising, overwhelming, raw, audacious, intimate novel this is. How masterful Ferrante's writing is, managing to unfold the complexities of life in a poor neighbourhood of Naples in the early 60's, but most importantly, the human complexities, especially of the two female protagonists, Lila and Elena (Lenu). <br/><br/>This second instalment picks up where it left off in the previous volume, with Lila being</span>
  <span id="freeText10490303163645764471" style="display:none">How does one even begin to review this second volume in the Neapolitan series?<br /><br />What an incredible, mesmerising, overwhelming, raw, audacious, intimate novel this is. How masterful Ferrante's writing is, managing to unfold the complexities of life in a poor neighbourhood of Naples in the early 60's, but most importantly, the human complexities, especially of the two female protagonists, Lila and Elena (Lenu). <br /><br />This second instalment picks up where it left off in the previous volume, with Lila being married at only sixteen with the grocer's son, Stefano Carracci, who's rich. Now Lila has what she and Lenu were dreaming of as kids - wealth. Will that bring her that elusive happiness or at least contentment? <br /><br />Nobody is more twisted, brilliant and difficult to understand than Lila. The fact that she's so beautiful only amplifies those traits. Women are jealous and hate her. Men want her. But they're only dazzled by her beauty. Beauty can be both a blessing and a curse.<br /><br />Elena (Lenu) is still our narrator. She studies hard, determined to get out of the neighbourhood. She only loves two people, Lila and the intelligent, brooding, Nino Sarratore. She wants his admiration and enjoys discussing politics and having high-level conversations with him. He's like no other men she's ever encountered. Or maybe he's not that different, after all ...<br /><br />There's so little and yet so much that goes on in this book. The narration is somewhat detached, but it carries so much weight, with incredibly nuanced emotions, thoughts and feelings. <br /><br />While no two people read and understand a book the same way, this novel is even more likely to be experienced differently by each and every reader - which makes it even more special.<br /><br />I personally am so enthralled by this book, it's almost overwhelming. <br /><br />I really don't have the words ...</span>
  <a data-text-id="10490303163645764471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1270678668" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1270678668" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1270678668?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781609451479" href="/book/show/19185150-the-story-of-a-new-name">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=disliked">disliked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270678668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16839605593788647651">Dear friends, how often is it that I dump a book? Don't I usually stick them out to the end? Well, I have had it with this one. It has not improved. I have gone over the half-way mark. Another 8.5 hours is unbearable. <br/><br/>In the first book of this series there is a friendship, albeit complicated. Now there is no semblance at all of any possible reason for friendship. You may think differently, but that is how I see it. I detest <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ Lila <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>! IF this is meant to be a coming-of</span>
  <span id="freeText16839605593788647651" style="display:none">Dear friends, how often is it that I dump a book? Don't I usually stick them out to the end? Well, I have had it with this one. It has not improved. I have gone over the half-way mark. Another 8.5 hours is unbearable. <br /><br />In the first book of this series there is a friendship, albeit complicated. Now there is no semblance at all of any possible reason for friendship. You may think differently, but that is how I see it. I detest <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Lila <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>! IF this is meant to be a coming-of-age-story, simply because it is about teenagers, I do not recommend it. Neither to adults nor a younger group of readers. I do not get a kick out of reading about nasty, mean people in my free time. I've had it.<br /><br />I can see no reason for continuing this novel. <br /><br />****************************<br /><br /> I am suffering with my book so I am going to complain a little. I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend" title="My Brilliant Friend by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">My Brilliant Friend</a> and gave it four stars so I figured I should continue with the next book in the series:<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18468568.The_Story_of_a_New_Name" title="The Story of a New Name by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">The Story of a New Name</a>.<br /><br />The second book is so very similar to the first! I have listened to 1/3 or a 1/2 of the book, something like that. Same theme again. Same message, and it is so unpleasant to follow. Two girlfriends. They admire and hate each other. Jealous. Nasty. Biting. Yes, the girls have it tough; both of them, but the book is not going anywhere.<br /><br />There are amusing lines like: "He is a student, but not too boring." <br /><br />Am I too old for this book? It is a coming-of-age story, of figuring out what you want to do with your life. At this point I just feel like shouting, "Figure it out. Make up your mind and stop being so dependent on each other."<br /><br />If I complain will it get better? If it does not improve I am certainly not going to read the third in the series. <br /><br />WARNING significant spoiler ahead: One more thing, the very <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[beginning of the first book is extremely important. It seems to say where this is all going to end up. So the surprise seems to be gone. Maybe I didn't hear that beginning correctly? This question is all that keeps an element of surprise to the book! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />BTW, the narration of the audiobook by Hillary Huber is excellent. It is not the narration that is the problem!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16839605593788647651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lDYTiA3WjaZ5Ofnqq/Eqi6Hgrxov+P/E+/aObJo1ftrg2SGKb2zK5sv+TeAP0EAYaBK3ZXkFl8eI8+t9x9EIXA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ewI1KeeKgcvSCbVjheuw6kKkWjmTDb9wuOkoHSV/N1oP7QcrhTDGi2DOAWkhytp5i1ZCRsXw13PL7E0MeJtB3A==')})
  }

Una casa, una famiglia, il passato, un\'idea di futuro, la nostra peggiore fotografia impressa a fuoco negli occhi di chi abbiamo amato. Passiamo la vita a spaccare vasi e incollare cocci illudendoci di essere nuovi di zecca. E cerchiamo di non guardare troppo indietro, perché il tempo dei bilanci è un tempo vano, ridicolo e struggente. Domenico Starnone ha scritto un libro intensissimo e vero. Il racconto a tre voci delle forze sotterranee che tengono in vita i matrimoni anche dopo l\'amore.\n\n«Se tu te ne sei scordato, egregio signore, te lo ricordo io: sono tua moglie». Si apre cosí la lettera che Vanda scrive al marito che se n\'è andato di casa, lasciandola in preda a una tempesta di rabbia impotente e domande che non trovano risposta. Si sono sposati giovani all\'inizio degli anni Sessanta, per desiderio di indipendenza, ma poi attorno a loro il mondo è cambiato, e ritrovarsi a trent\'anni con una famiglia a carico è diventato un segno di arretratezza piú che di autonomia. Perciò adesso lui se ne sta a Roma, innamorato della grazia lieve di una sconosciuta con cui i giorni sono sempre gioiosi, e lei a Napoli con i figli, a misurare l\'estensione del silenzio e il crescere dell\'estraneità. Che cosa siamo disposti a sacrificare, pur di non sentirci in trappola? E che cosa perdiamo, quando scegliamo di tornare sui nostri passi? Perché niente è piú radicale dell\'abbandono, ma niente è piú tenace di quei lacci invisibili che legano le persone le une alle altre. E a volte basta un gesto minimo per far riaffiorare quello che abbiamo provato a mettere da parte. There is kind, handsome Willem, an aspiring actor; JB, a quick-witted, sometimes cruel Brooklyn-born painter seeking entry to the art world; Malcolm, a frustrated architect at a prominent firm; and withdrawn, brilliant, enigmatic Jude, who serves as their center of gravity. \n\nOver the decades, their relationships deepen and darken, tinged by addiction, success, and pride. Yet their greatest challenge, each comes to realize, is Jude himself, by midlife a terrifyingly talented litigator yet an increasingly broken man, his mind and body scarred by an unspeakable childhood, and haunted by what he fears is a degree of trauma that he’ll not only be unable to overcome—but that will define his life forever.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11853076401360812156\" href=\"#\" Though her own circumstances remain indistinct, she becomes the audience to a chain of narratives, as the people she meets tell her one after another the stories of their lives.\n\nBeginning with the neighbouring passenger on the flight out and his tales of fast boats and failed marriages, the storytellers talk of their loves and ambitions and pains, their anxieties, their perceptions and daily lives. In the stifling heat and noise of the city the sequence of voice begins to weave a complex human tapestry. The more they talk the more elliptical their listener becomes, as she shapes and directs their accounts until certain themes begin to emerge: the experience of loss, the nature of family life, the difficulty of intimacy and the mystery of creativity itself.\n\nOutline is a novel about writing and talking, about self-effacement and self-expression, about the desire to create and the human art of <span id="freeTextContainer16440976446958057850">Elena Ferrante is a pseudonymous Italian novelist. Ferrante's books, originally published in Italian, have been translated into many languages. Her four-book series of Neapolitan Novels are her most widely known works.</span>

Through it all, the women’s friendship has remained the gravitational center of their lives. \n\nBoth women once fought to escape the neighborhood in which they grew up—a prison of conformity, violence, and inviolable taboos. Elena married, moved to Florence, started a family, and published several well-received books. In this final book, she has returned to Naples. Lila, on the other hand, never succeeded in freeing herself from the city of her birth. She has become a successful entrepreneur, but her success draws her into closer proximity with the nepotism, chauvinism, and criminal violence that infect her neighborhood. Proximity to the world she has always rejected only brings her role as its unacknowledged leader into relief.
